## Conversation

### User (Turn 1)

<command-message>teach</command-message>
<command-name>/teach</command-name>

### Assistant (Turn 2)

Base directory for this skill: /home/douglas-gubert/.claude-personal/skills/teach

The user has asked you to teach them something. This is a stateful request - they intend to learn the topic over multiple sessions.

## Teaching Workspace

Treat the current directory as a teaching workspace. The state of their learning is captured in this directory in several files:

- `MISSION.md`: A document capturing the _reason_ the user is interested in the topic. This should be used to ground all teaching. Use the format in [MISSION-FORMAT.md](./MISSION-FORMAT.md).
- `./reference/*.html`: A directory of reference materials. These are the compressed learnings from the lessons - cheat sheets, reference algorithms, syntax, yoga poses, glossaries. They are the raw units of learning. They should be beautiful documents which print out well, and are designed for quick reference.
- `RESOURCES.md`: A list of resources which can be explored to ground your teaching in contextual knowledge, or to acquire knowledge and wisdom. Use the format in [RESOURCES-FORMAT.md](./RESOURCES-FORMAT.md).
- `./learning-records/*.md`: A directory of learning records, which capture what the user has learned. These are loosely equivalent to architectural decision records in software development - they capture non-obvious lessons and key insights that may need to be revised later, or drive future sessions. These should be used to calculate the zone of proximal development. They are titled `0001-<dash-case-name>.md`, where the number increments each time. Use the format in [LEARNING-RECORD-FORMAT.md](./LEARNING-RECORD-FORMAT.md).
- `./lessons/*.html`: A directory of lessons. A **lesson** is a single, self-contained HTML output that teaches one tightly-scoped thing tied to the mission. This is the primary unit of teaching in this workspace.
- `./assets/*`: Reusable **components** shared across lessons. See [Assets](#assets).
- `NOTES.md`: A scratchpad for you to jot down user preferences, or working notes.

## Philosophy

To learn at a deep level, the user needs three things:

- **Knowledge**, captured from high-quality, high-trust resources
- **Skills**, acquired through highly-relevant interactive lessons devised by you, based on the knowledge
- **Wisdom**, which comes from interacting with other learners and practitioners

Before the `RESOURCES.md` is well-populated, your focus should be to find high-quality resources which will help the user acquire knowledge. Never trust your parametric knowledge.

Some topics may require more skills than knowledge. Learning more about theoretical physics might be more knowledge-based. For yoga, more skills-based.

### Fluency vs Storage Strength

You should be careful to split between two types of learning:

- **Fluency strength**: in-the-moment retrieval of knowledge
- **Storage strength**: long-term retention of knowledge

Fluency can give the user an illusory sense of mastery, but storage strength is the real goal. Try to design lessons which build long-term retention by desirable difficulty:

- Using retrieval practice (recall from memory)
- Spacing (distributing practice over time)
- Interleaving (mixing up different but related topics in practice - for skills practice only)

## Lessons

A lesson is the main thing you produce — the unit in which knowledge and skills reach the user. Each lesson is one self-contained HTML file, saved to `./lessons/` and titled `0001-<dash-case-name>.html` where the number increments each time.

A lesson should be **beautiful** — clean, readable typography and layout — since the user will return to these later to review. Think Tufte.

The lesson should be short, and completable very quickly. Learners' working memory is very small, and we need to stay within it. But each lesson should give the user a single tangible win that they can build on. It should be directly tied to the mission, and should be in the user's zone of proximal development.

If possible, open the lesson file for the user by running a CLI command.

Each lesson should link via HTML anchors to other lessons and reference documents.

Each lesson should recommend a primary source for the user to read or watch. This should be the most high-quality, high-trust resource you found on the topic.

Each lesson should contain a reminder to ask followup questions to the agent. The agent is their teacher, and can assist with anything that's unclear.

## Assets

Lessons are built from reusable **components**, stored in `./assets/`: stylesheets, quiz widgets, simulators, diagram helpers — anything a second lesson could reuse.

Reuse is the default, not the exception. Before authoring a lesson, read `./assets/` and build from the components already there. When a lesson needs something new and reusable, write it as a component in `./assets/` and link to it — never inline code a future lesson would duplicate.

A shared stylesheet is the first component every workspace earns: every lesson links it, so the lessons look like one consistent course rather than a pile of one-offs. As the workspace grows, so should the component library.

## The Mission

Every lesson should be tied into the mission - the reason that the user is interested in learning about the topic.

If the user is unclear about the mission, or the `MISSION.md` is not populated, your first job should be to question the user on why they want to learn this.

Failing to understand the mission will mean knowledge acquisition is not grounded in real-world goals. Lessons will feel too abstract. You will have no way of judging what the user should do next.

Missions may change as the user develops more skills and knowledge. This is normal - make sure to update the `MISSION.md` and add a learning record to capture the change. Confirm with the user before changing the mission.

## Zone Of Proximal Development

Each lesson, the user should always feel as if they are being challenged 'just enough'.

The user may specify an exact thing they want to learn. If they don't, figure out their zone of proximal development by:

- Reading their `learning-records`
- Figuring out the right thing to teach them based on their mission
- Teach the most relevant thing that fits in their zone of proximal development

## Knowledge

Lessons should be designed around a skill the user is going to learn. The knowledge in the lesson should be only what's required to acquire that skill. You teach the knowledge first, then get the user to practice the skills via an interactive feedback loop.

Knowledge should first be gathered from trusted resources. Use `RESOURCES.md` to keep track of them. Lessons should be littered with citations - links to external resources to back up any claim made. This increases the trustworthiness of the lesson.

For acquiring knowledge, difficulty is the enemy. It eats working memory you need for understanding.

## Skills

If knowledge is all about acquisition, skills are about durability and flexibility. Make the knowledge stick.

For skill acquisition, difficulty is the tool. Effortful retrieval is what builds storage strength. Skills should be taught through interactive lessons. There are several tools at your disposal:

- Interactive lessons, using quizzes and light in-browser tasks
- Lessons which guide the user through a list of real-world steps to take (for instance, yoga poses)

Each of these should be based on a **feedback loop**, where the user receives feedback on their performance. This feedback loop should be as tight as possible, giving feedback immediately - and ideally automatically.

For quizzes, each answer should be exactly the same number of words (and characters, if possible). Don't give the user any clues about the answer through formatting.

## Acquiring Wisdom

Wisdom comes from true real-world interaction - testing your skills outside the learning environment.

When the user asks a question that appears to require wisdom, your default posture should be to attempt to answer - but to ultimately delegate to a **community**.

A community is a place (online or offline) where the user can test their skills in the real world. This might be a forum, a subreddit, a real-world class (budget permitting) or a local interest group.

You should attempt to find high-reputation communities the user can join. If the user expresses a preference that they don't want to join a community, respect it.

## Reference Documents

While creating lessons, you should also create reference documents. Lessons can reference these documents - they are useful for tracking raw units of knowledge useful across lessons.

Lessons will rarely be revisited later - reference documents will be. They should be the compressed essence of the lesson, in a format designed for quick reference.

Some learning topics lend themselves to reference:

- Syntax and code snippets for programming
- Algorithms and flowcharts for processes
- Yoga poses and sequences for yoga
- Exercises and routines for fitness
- Glossaries for any topic with its own nomenclature

Glossaries, in particular, are an essential reference. Once one is created, it should be adhered to in every lesson.

## `NOTES.md`

The user will sometimes express preferences of how they want to be taught, or things you should keep in mind. This is the place to record those preferences, so you can refer back to them when designing lessons or working with the user.


### User (Turn 3)

<command-name>/teach</command-name>
<command-args>I want to learn about the fuzzy search algorithm. Break it down into independent, increasingly complex tasks that allow me to fully grasp how the decisions taken in the algorithm solve specific aspects of the proposed problem</command-args>

1	# RESOURCES.md Format
2	
3	`RESOURCES.md` is the curated set of trusted sources for this topic. Knowledge for explainers should be drawn from here, not from parametric guesses. Wisdom comes from the communities listed here.
4	
5	## Structure
6	
7	```md
8	# {Topic} Resources
9	
10	## Knowledge
11	
12	- [Book: _The Science and Practice of Strength Training_ — Zatsiorsky & Kraemer](https://example.com)
13	  Foundational text on programming and adaptation. Use for: anything to do with periodisation, recovery, intensity zones.
14	- [Article: "How Much Should I Train?" — Greg Nuckols (Stronger By Science)](https://example.com)
15	  Evidence-based review of volume landmarks. Use for: weekly set targets per muscle group.
16	
17	## Wisdom (Communities)
18	
19	- [r/weightroom](https://reddit.com/r/weightroom)
20	  High-signal subreddit, moderated against bro-science. Use for: programme critique, plateau troubleshooting.
21	- Local: Tuesday strength class at {gym name}
22	  Use for: real-time coaching feedback on lifts.
23	```
24	
25	## Rules
26	
27	- **High-trust only.** Prefer primary sources, recognised experts, peer-reviewed work, and communities with strong moderation. If a resource is marketing dressed as education, leave it out.
28	- **Annotate every entry.** A bare link is useless in three months. Add one line: what it covers and when to reach for it.
29	- **Group by Knowledge / Wisdom.** Mirrors the philosophy in [SKILL.md](./SKILL.md). It is fine for a resource to appear in only one group.
30	- **Surface gaps explicitly.** If no good resource exists for an area the mission needs, write a `## Gaps` section listing what is missing. This drives future search.
31	- **Prune ruthlessly.** A resource that turned out to be wrong, shallow, or off-mission should be removed, not buried. Better five sharp sources than thirty mediocre ones.
32	- **Record community preferences.** If the user has opted out of joining communities, note it here so future sessions don't keep proposing them.
33	

1	# MISSION.md Format
2	
3	`MISSION.md` lives at the workspace root. It captures the _reason_ the user is learning this topic. Every teaching decision — what to teach next, which resources to surface, which exercises to design — should trace back to this document.
4	
5	## Template
6	
7	```md
8	# Mission: {Topic}
9	
10	## Why
11	{1-3 sentences. The concrete real-world goal the user is chasing. What changes in their life or work when they have this skill? Avoid abstract framings like "to understand X" — push for the underlying outcome.}
12	
13	## Success looks like
14	- {A specific, observable thing the user will be able to do}
15	- {Another specific thing}
16	- {…}
17	
18	## Constraints
19	- {Time, budget, prior commitments, learning preferences, anything that bounds the approach}
20	
21	## Out of scope
22	- {Adjacent topics the user explicitly does not want to chase right now — protects the zone of proximal development}
23	```
24	
25	## Rules
26	
27	- **One mission per workspace.** If the user wants to learn two unrelated things, that is two workspaces.
28	- **Concrete over abstract.** "Run a half marathon by October" beats "get fitter." "Ship a Rust CLI to my team" beats "learn Rust."
29	- **Push back on vagueness.** If the user cannot articulate why, interview them before writing anything. A bad mission is worse than no mission.
30	- **Revise when reality shifts.** Missions change. When the user's goal moves, update this file — don't leave a stale mission steering future sessions.
31	- **Keep it short.** If `MISSION.md` runs past a screen, it has stopped being a compass and started being a plan.
32	

1	# Learning Record Format
2	
3	Learning records live in `./learning-records/` and use sequential numbering: `0001-slug.md`, `0002-slug.md`, etc. Create the directory lazily — only when the first record is written.
4	
5	They are the teaching equivalent of ADRs: they capture non-obvious lessons, key insights, and stated prior knowledge that will steer future sessions. They are used to calculate the zone of proximal development.
6	
7	## Template
8	
9	```md
10	# {Short title of what was learned or established}
11	
12	{1-3 sentences: what was learned (or what prior knowledge was established), and why it matters for future sessions.}
13	```
14	
15	That is the whole format. A learning record can be a single paragraph. The value is recording _that_ this is now known and _why_ it changes what to teach next — not in filling out sections.
16	
17	## Optional sections
18	
19	Only include these when they add genuine value. Most records won't need them.
20	
21	- **Status** frontmatter (`active | superseded by LR-NNNN`) — useful when an earlier understanding turns out to be wrong and is replaced.
22	- **Evidence** — how the user demonstrated the understanding (a question answered, an exercise completed, prior experience cited). Useful when the claim might be revisited.
23	- **Implications** — what this unlocks or rules out for future sessions. Worth recording when non-obvious.
24	
25	## Numbering
26	
27	Scan `./learning-records/` for the highest existing number and increment by one.
28	
29	## When to write a learning record
30	
31	Write one when any of these is true:
32	
33	1. **The user demonstrated genuine understanding of something non-trivial** — not just exposure, but evidence they can use the concept correctly. This sets a new floor for what to teach next.
34	2. **The user disclosed prior knowledge** — "I already know X." Record it so future sessions don't re-teach it. Also record the _depth_ claimed.
35	3. **A misconception was corrected** — the user previously believed something wrong and now sees why. These are high-value: they predict future stumbling blocks for related topics.
36	4. **The mission shifted in response to learning** — the user discovered they cared about something different than they thought. Cross-link to [[MISSION.md]] and update it.
37	
38	### What does _not_ qualify
39	
40	- Material that was merely covered. Coverage is not learning. Wait for evidence.
41	- Anything already captured tersely in [[GLOSSARY.md]] as a term definition. Don't duplicate.
42	- Session-by-session activity logs. Learning records are not a journal — they are decision-grade insights.
43	
44	## Supersession
45	
46	When a later record contradicts an earlier one (the user's understanding deepened or corrected), mark the old record `Status: superseded by LR-NNNN` rather than deleting it. The history of how understanding evolved is itself useful signal.
47

I found comprehensive information about fuzzy search algorithms. Here's what the search revealed:

## Overview of Fuzzy Search Algorithms

Fuzzy string searching is the technique of finding strings that match a pattern approximately (rather than exactly).

## Key Algorithms

**Levenshtein Distance:**
This algorithm measures the similarity between two strings by determining the minimum number of single-character edits (insertions, deletions, or substitutions) required to change one string into the other.

**Bitap Algorithm:**
The bitap algorithm (also known as the 'shift-or' or 'shift-and' algorithm) is very efficient for relatively short pattern strings. The Bitap algorithm uses bitwise operations on bitmasks which are extremely fast. Bitap can efficiently find all places in source text that are at most k edits away from the search string, where "edits" are in terms of Levenshtein distance.

## FZF Scoring

fzy treats scoring as a modified edit distance problem of calculating the Levenshtein distance, where edit distance is the measure of how different two strings are in terms of insertions, deletions, and substitutions. fzy's scoring is a dynamic programming algorithm similar to Wagner–Fischer and Needleman–Wunsch. fzy builds an n-by-m matrix where each position stores the score for matching the first i characters of the search with the first j characters of the candidate.

A common command-line tool fzf is often used to integrate approximate string searching into various command-line applications.


REMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks.

I've completed the web search for fuzzy string matching algorithm design decisions and approximate string search. Here are the key findings:

## Overview

Approximate string matching (often colloquially referred to as fuzzy string searching) is the technique of finding strings that match a pattern approximately (rather than exactly).

## Core Algorithm Categories

Approximate string matching algorithms are traditionally classified into two categories: online and offline. With online algorithms the pattern can be processed before searching but the text cannot, meaning online techniques do searching without an index.

## Key Algorithms

Several algorithms are implemented for approximate string matchings — Levenshtein, Damerau-Levenshtein, Bitap and n-gram. The Bitap algorithm is an approximate string matching algorithm that indicates whether a given text contains a substring "approximately equal" to a given pattern, where approximate equality arises in terms of Levenshtein distance.

## Distance Metrics

The closeness of a match is measured in terms of the number of primitive operations necessary to convert the string into an exact match, a number called the edit distance between the string and the pattern.

## Design Considerations

Teams should start with a threshold based on expected error rate—most teams measure edit distance as a percentage of string length rather than using a fixed number, then refine by testing against a labeled dataset and tracking precision versus recall.

## Practical Applications

Fuzzy string matching is widely used in spell checkers, de-duplication of records, master data management, plagiarism detection, bioinformatics and DNA sequencing, spam filtering, and content searches.

I've completed the web search for "Levenshtein distance dynamic programming intuition tutorial beginner". Here's a summary of what I found:

## Overview

Levenshtein distance is a measure of the similarity between two strings, which takes into account the number of insertion, deletion and substitution operations needed to transform one string into the other.

## Dynamic Programming Approach

Computing the Levenshtein distance is based on the observation that if we reserve a matrix to hold the Levenshtein distances between all prefixes of the first string and all prefixes of the second, then we can compute the values in the matrix in a dynamic programming fashion.

Dynamic programming is the recursive analysis of simpler subproblems. We save time by breaking down the problem into smaller parts, and then we use the solutions of our smaller sub-problems to build a map that helps us efficiently find our way to the answer.

## Key Intuition

The iterative solution is the "bottom up." We'll start filling out the chart by filling out each cell, row-by-row. Once we have the bottom-right value, we have our Levenshtein distance.

The matrix is typically initialized with incremental values along its first row and column, representing the cost of converting an empty string to the characters of the other string. As the matrix is filled row by row and column by column, each cell is computed based on a recurrence relation that compares the current characters and derives the minimum cost from previous cells. This process creates a dynamic programming grid where the bottom-right cell holds the final similarity score or distance between the full strings.

## Example

To convert "kitten" to "sitting": kitten → sitten (substitution), sitten → sittin (substitution), sittin → sitting (insertion). In this case it took three operations, so the levenshtein distance will be 3.

The search results include resources from GeeksforGeeks, Baeldung, Wikipedia, and other educational platforms that provide code examples and detailed explanations suitable for beginners.

As seen on GitHub.com! · GitHub","url":"https://github.com/jhawthorn/fzy.js/"},{"title":"GitHub - swarn/fzy-lua: A lua implementation of the fzy fuzzy matching algorithm · GitHub","url":"https://github.com/swarn/fzy-lua"},{"title":"fzy-lua/docs/fzy.md at main · swarn/fzy-lua","url":"https://github.com/swarn/fzy-lua/blob/main/docs/fzy.md"},{"title":"fzy.js/README.md at main · jhawthorn/fzy.js","url":"https://github.com/jhawthorn/fzy.js/blob/main/README.md"},{"title":"GitHub - hugopl/fzy: A Crystal port of awesome Fzy project, a fuzzy finder algorithm.","url":"https://github.com/hugopl/fzy"}]

Based on the search results, here's what I found about fzy's scoring algorithm using dynamic programming and consecutive bonus:

## Dynamic Programming Approach

Dynamic programming requires the observation that the result is based on the result of subproblems. Fzy borrows heavily from concepts in bioinformatics to performs scoring. Fzy builds a n-by-m matrix, where n is the length of the search string and m the length of the candidate string. Each position (i,j) in the matrix stores the score for matching the first i characters of the search with the first j characters of the candidate.

## Gap Penalty and Consecutive Match Bonus

Fzy calculates an affine gap penalty, this means simply that we assign a constant penalty for having a gap and a linear penalty for the length of the gap. Inspired by the Gotoh algorithm, fzy computes a second D (for diagonal) matrix in parallel with the score matrix. The D matrix computes the best score which ends in a match. This allows both computation of the penalty for starting a gap and the score for a consecutive match.

## Scoring Philosophy

It does this by favouring matches on consecutive letters and starts of words.

Input: {
  "file_path": "/work/random/ai-teach-test/lessons/0001-the-problem.html",
  "content": "<!DOCTYPE html>\n<html lang=\"en\">\n<head>\n  <meta charset=\"UTF-8\">\n  <meta name=\"viewport\" content=\"width=device-width, initial-scale=1.0\">\n  <title>Lesson 1 — The Problem: Why Exact Search Fails</title>\n  <link rel=\"stylesheet\" href=\"../assets/style.css\">\n</head>\n<body>\n\n<div class=\"lesson-header\">\n  <div class=\"lesson-meta\">Fuzzy Search · Lesson 1 of 6</div>\n  <h1>The Problem: Why Exact Search Fails</h1>\n  <p class=\"lesson-subtitle\">Understanding what we are trying to solve before we solve it</p>\n</div>\n\n<h2>The promise of exact search</h2>\n\n<p>\n  The simplest search algorithm imaginable is a direct string comparison:\n  does the candidate contain the query as a substring? This is what\n  <code>str.includes(query)</code>, <code>grep</code>, and <code>LIKE '%…%'</code> all do.\n</p>\n\n<p>\n  It is fast, predictable, and sufficient — until the moment a human is\n  doing the typing.\n</p>\n\n<div class=\"callout note\">\n  <span class=\"callout-label\">Core insight</span>\n  Exact match assumes the query is a precise, correctly-spelled substring of\n  the target. Human queries almost never are.\n</div>\n\n<h2>Three ways humans fail to type what they mean</h2>\n\n<h3>1. Typos and transpositions</h3>\n<p>\n  A developer types <code>comit</code> instead of <code>commit</code>.\n  They type <code>recieve</code> instead of <code>receive</code>.\n  Exact match returns nothing. But the intent is unambiguous.\n</p>\n\n<h3>2. Abbreviations</h3>\n<p>\n  A user types <code>usr/lcl</code> to find <code>/usr/local/bin</code>.\n  They type <code>MC</code> to find <code>MyComponent.tsx</code>.\n  No substring match exists, but the intent is clear: the query characters\n  all appear in the target, in order.\n</p>\n\n<h3>3. Fuzzy recall</h3>\n<p>\n  A user remembers a file is \"something about authentication, starts with\n  auth, has config in the name.\" They type <code>authcfg</code>.\n  The file is named <code>auth_config_loader.ts</code>.\n  Again: exact match fails, human intent succeeds.\n</p>\n\n<div class=\"demo\" id=\"demo-exact\">\n  <h3>Try it: Exact substring search</h3>\n  <p style=\"font-family:var(--sans);font-size:0.88rem;color:var(--muted);margin-bottom:0.75rem;\">\n    Type a query. See which candidates match using exact substring search.\n  </p>\n  <input type=\"text\" id=\"exact-input\" placeholder=\"try: comit, authcfg, usr/lcl\" autocomplete=\"off\" spellcheck=\"false\">\n  <div class=\"demo-results\" id=\"exact-results\"></div>\n</div>\n\n<h2>What the problem really is</h2>\n\n<p>\n  The challenge has two distinct parts, and most confusion about fuzzy search\n  comes from conflating them:\n</p>\n\n<ol>\n  <li>\n    <strong>Matching</strong> — deciding whether a candidate is \"close enough\"\n    to the query at all.\n  </li>\n  <li>\n    <strong>Ranking</strong> — when multiple candidates pass the threshold,\n    deciding which one the user probably meant.\n  </li>\n</ol>\n\n<p>\n  These require different machinery. A good fuzzy search algorithm solves\n  <em>both</em> — and the rest of this course shows how each design decision\n  targets one or the other.\n</p>\n\n<div class=\"callout insight\">\n  <span class=\"callout-label\">Preview of what's coming</span>\n  <strong>Edit distance</strong> solves matching: it gives us a number\n  representing how \"far apart\" two strings are. <strong>Scoring models</strong>\n  (gap penalties, consecutive bonuses, boundary bonuses) solve ranking:\n  they let us order results by likely intent.\n</div>\n\n<h2>What properties must a solution have?</h2>\n\n<p>Before building anything, it helps to list the requirements the solution\nmust satisfy. A fuzzy search algorithm should:</p>\n\n<ul>\n  <li><strong>Tolerate small typos</strong> — off-by-one character edits should not disqualify a match.</li>\n  <li><strong>Support abbreviations</strong> — all query characters appear in the target, in order (subsequence matching).</li>\n  <li><strong>Rank better matches first</strong> — <code>comit</code> → <code>commit</code> should rank above <code>comit</code> → <code>recommit_changes_v2.log</code>.</li>\n  <li><strong>Prefer compact matches</strong> — matching 4 consecutive characters should score higher than the same 4 characters scattered across a 200-character string.</li>\n  <li><strong>Prefer early and boundary matches</strong> — a match at the start of a word or path component feels more intentional than one buried in the middle.</li>\n</ul>\n\n<div class=\"callout warn\">\n  <span class=\"callout-label\">The tradeoff</span>\n  Every relaxation of exact match increases the number of results returned\n  (recall goes up). The scoring model is what keeps <em>precision</em> usable\n  by surfacing the right result first.\n</div>\n\n<h2>Quiz: Check your understanding</h2>\n\n<div class=\"quiz\" id=\"q1\">\n  <p class=\"quiz-question\">Q1. Exact substring search on the query <code>authcfg</code> against the target <code>auth_config_loader.ts</code> returns no match. What property does a fuzzy search algorithm need to find it?</p>\n  <ul class=\"quiz-options\">\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q1\" value=\"a\"> Tolerance for transpositions only</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q1\" value=\"b\"> Subsequence matching — all query chars appear in the target in order</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q1\" value=\"c\"> Phonetic similarity between query and target</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q1\" value=\"d\"> Case-insensitive exact matching</label></li>\n  </ul>\n  <button class=\"btn btn-primary\" onclick=\"checkQ('q1','b','Correct. The characters a-u-t-h-c-f-g all appear in auth_config_loader.ts in that order. This is subsequence matching — a core building block of fuzzy search.')\">Check</button>\n  <p class=\"quiz-feedback\" id=\"q1-feedback\"></p>\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"quiz\" id=\"q2\">\n  <p class=\"quiz-question\">Q2. Two candidates both pass the fuzzy match threshold for query <code>cfg</code>: <code>cfg.ts</code> and <code>app_configuration_manager.ts</code>. Which problem does the <em>scoring model</em> solve here?</p>\n  <ul class=\"quiz-options\">\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q2\" value=\"a\"> It decides whether either candidate matches at all</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q2\" value=\"b\"> It computes how many characters differ between query and candidate</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q2\" value=\"c\"> It ranks the candidates so the more likely intended result appears first</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q2\" value=\"d\"> It removes duplicate matches from the result set</label></li>\n  </ul>\n  <button class=\"btn btn-primary\" onclick=\"checkQ('q2','c','Correct. Matching decides yes/no. Scoring decides who comes first. cfg.ts should rank above app_configuration_manager.ts because the match is more compact and starts at a word boundary.')\">Check</button>\n  <p class=\"quiz-feedback\" id=\"q2-feedback\"></p>\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"quiz\" id=\"q3\">\n  <p class=\"quiz-question\">Q3. A fuzzy search that is very permissive (accepts almost anything) will have high recall but low precision. What is the role of the scoring model in addressing this tradeoff?</p>\n  <ul class=\"quiz-options\">\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q3\" value=\"a\"> It raises the match threshold to reduce the number of results</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q3\" value=\"b\"> It surfaces the most likely intended result first, making high recall usable</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q3\" value=\"c\"> It replaces the match threshold entirely</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q3\" value=\"d\"> It reduces the length of candidates to improve comparison speed</label></li>\n  </ul>\n  <button class=\"btn btn-primary\" onclick=\"checkQ('q3','b','Correct. You keep recall high so nothing is missed, but scoring ensures position #1 is almost always right. The user does not have to wade through noise.')\">Check</button>\n  <p class=\"quiz-feedback\" id=\"q3-feedback\"></p>\n</div>\n\n<hr>\n\n<h2>The curriculum ahead</h2>\n\n<p>Each of the next five lessons targets one specific design decision and the problem it solves:</p>\n\n<ul class=\"curriculum\">\n  <li><span class=\"num current\">01</span> <span class=\"current\">The Problem — why exact match fails (this lesson)</span></li>\n  <li><span class=\"num\">02</span> <a href=\"0002-edit-distance.html\">Edit Distance — formalising \"closeness\" with Levenshtein distance</a></li>\n  <li><span class=\"num locked\">03</span> <span class=\"locked\">Computing Edit Distance — the DP table and recurrence relation</span></li>\n  <li><span class=\"num locked\">04</span> <span class=\"locked\">From Distance to Search — threshold filtering and normalisation</span></li>\n  <li><span class=\"num locked\">05</span> <span class=\"locked\">The Scoring Model — gap penalties, consecutive and boundary bonuses (fzy)</span></li>\n  <li><span class=\"num locked\">06</span> <span class=\"locked\">The Bitap Algorithm — bit-parallel matching for performance</span></li>\n</ul>\n\n<div class=\"primary-source\">\n  <span class=\"label\">Primary source for this lesson</span>\n  <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Approximate_string_matching\" target=\"_blank\">\n    Approximate string matching — Wikipedia\n  </a>\n  — Read the intro and \"Problem formulation\" sections. It frames the\n  problem space precisely and introduces the vocabulary used in all subsequent\n  algorithm literature.\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"ask-teacher\">\n  💬 <strong>Have a question?</strong> Ask your teacher directly — type your question in the chat and I'll explain, rephrase, or go deeper on anything from this lesson.\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"lesson-nav\">\n  <span></span>\n  <span class=\"center\">Fuzzy Search · Lesson 1 of 6</span>\n  <a href=\"0002-edit-distance.html\">Next: Edit Distance →</a>\n</div>\n\n<script>\n  // ── Exact-search demo ──────────────────────────────────\n  const CANDIDATES = [\n    'commit',\n    'auth_config_loader.ts',\n    '/usr/local/bin',\n    'MyComponent.tsx',\n    'receive',\n    'recommit_changes_v2.log',\n    'cfg.ts',\n    'app_configuration_manager.ts',\n  ];\n\n  const exactInput   = document.getElementById('exact-input');\n  const exactResults = document.getElementById('exact-results');\n\n  function runExact(query) {\n    if (!query) { exactResults.innerHTML = ''; return; }\n    const q = query.toLowerCase();\n    const lines = CANDIDATES.map(c => {\n      const hit = c.toLowerCase().includes(q);\n      const escaped = c.replace(/</g, '&lt;');\n      if (hit) {\n        const highlighted = escaped.replace(\n          new RegExp(q.replace(/[.*+?^${}()|[\\]\\\\]/g, '\\\\$&'), 'gi'),\n          m => `<span class=\"hit\">${m}</span>`\n        );\n        return `<div class=\"match\">✓ ${highlighted}</div>`;\n      }\n      return `<div class=\"no-match\">✗ ${escaped}</div>`;\n    });\n    exactResults.innerHTML = lines.join('');\n  }\n\n  exactInput.addEventListener('input', e => runExact(e.target.value));\n\n  // ── Quiz logic ─────────────────────────────────────────\n  function checkQ(id, correct, explanation) {\n    const selected = document.querySelector(`input[name=\"${id}\"]:checked`);\n    const feedback = document.getElementById(`${id}-feedback`);\n    if (!selected) { feedback.textContent = 'Please select an answer.'; feedback.className = 'quiz-feedback'; return; }\n\n    document.querySelectorAll(`input[name=\"${id}\"]`).forEach(r => {\n      const lbl = r.closest('label');\n      if (r.value === correct) lbl.classList.add('correct');\n      else if (r.checked)      lbl.classList.add('wrong');\n      r.disabled = true;\n    });\n\n    if (selected.value === correct) {\n      feedback.textContent = '✓ ' + explanation;\n      feedback.className = 'quiz-feedback correct';\n    } else {\n      feedback.textContent = '✗ Not quite. ' + explanation;\n      feedback.className = 'quiz-feedback wrong';\n    }\n  }\n</script>\n\n</body>\n</html>\n"
}

| # | Problem | Solution |
|---|---------|---------|
| 1 | Exact match fails for typos, abbreviations, fuzzy recall | Frame the requirements |
| 2 | We need to measure "closeness" formally | Levenshtein edit distance |

| 4 | Distance gives a number, not a yes/no match | Threshold filtering & normalisation |

1	# MISSION.md Format
2	
3	`MISSION.md` lives at the workspace root. It captures the _reason_ the user is learning this topic. Every teaching decision — what to teach next, which resources to surface, which exercises to design — should trace back to this document.
4	
5	## Template
6	
7	```md
8	# Mission: {Topic}
9	
10	## Why
11	{1-3 sentences. The concrete real-world goal the user is chasing. What changes in their life or work when they have this skill? Avoid abstract framings like "to understand X" — push for the underlying outcome.}
12	
13	## Success looks like
14	- {A specific, observable thing the user will be able to do}
15	- {Another specific thing}
16	- {…}
17	
18	## Constraints
19	- {Time, budget, prior commitments, learning preferences, anything that bounds the approach}
20	
21	## Out of scope
22	- {Adjacent topics the user explicitly does not want to chase right now — protects the zone of proximal development}
23	```
24	
25	## Rules
26	
27	- **One mission per workspace.** If the user wants to learn two unrelated things, that is two workspaces.
28	- **Concrete over abstract.** "Run a half marathon by October" beats "get fitter." "Ship a Rust CLI to my team" beats "learn Rust."
29	- **Push back on vagueness.** If the user cannot articulate why, interview them before writing anything. A bad mission is worse than no mission.
30	- **Revise when reality shifts.** Missions change. When the user's goal moves, update this file — don't leave a stale mission steering future sessions.
31	- **Keep it short.** If `MISSION.md` runs past a screen, it has stopped being a compass and started being a plan.
32	
```

I found comprehensive information about fuzzy search algorithms. Here's what the search revealed:

## Overview of Fuzzy Search Algorithms

Fuzzy string searching is the technique of finding strings that match a pattern approximately (rather than exactly).

## Key Algorithms

**Levenshtein Distance:**
This algorithm measures the similarity between two strings by determining the minimum number of single-character edits (insertions, deletions, or substitutions) required to change one string into the other.

**Bitap Algorithm:**
The bitap algorithm (also known as the 'shift-or' or 'shift-and' algorithm) is very efficient for relatively short pattern strings. The Bitap algorithm uses bitwise operations on bitmasks which are extremely fast. Bitap can efficiently find all places in source text that are at most k edits away from the search string, where "edits" are in terms of Levenshtein distance.

##...
```

Based on the search results, here's what I found about fzy's scoring algorithm using dynamic programming and consecutive bonus:

## Dynamic Programming Approach

Dynamic programming requires the observation that the result is based on the result of subproblems. Fzy borrows heavily from concepts in bioinformatics to performs scoring. Fzy builds a n-by-m matrix, where n is the length of the search string and m the length of the candidate string. Each position (i,j) in the matrix stores the score for matching the first i characters of the search with the first j characters of the candidate.

## Gap Penalty and Consecutive Match Bonus

Fzy calculates an affine gap penalty, this means simply that we assign a constant penalty for having a gap and a linear penalty for the length of the gap. Inspired by the Gotoh algorith...

```json
{
  "file_path": "/work/random/ai-teach-test/lessons/0001-the-problem.html",
  "content": "<!DOCTYPE html>\n<html lang=\"en\">\n<head>\n  <meta charset=\"UTF-8\">\n  <meta name=\"viewport\" content=\"width=device-width, initial-scale=1.0\">\n  <title>Lesson 1 — The Problem: Why Exact Search Fails</title>\n  <link rel=\"stylesheet\" href=\"../assets/style.css\">\n</head>\n<body>\n\n<div class=\"lesson-header\">\n  <div class=\"lesson-meta\">Fuzzy Search · Lesson 1 of 6</div>\n  <h1>The Problem: Why Exact Search Fails</h1>\n  <p class=\"lesson-subtitle\">Understanding what we are trying to solve before we solve it</p>\n</div>\n\n<h2>The promise of exact search</h2>\n\n<p>\n  The simplest search algorithm imaginable is a direct string comparison:\n  does the candidate contain the query as a substring? This is what\n  <code>str.includes(query)</code>, <code>grep</code>, and <code>LIKE '%…%'</code> all do.\n</p>\n\n<p>\n  It is fast, predictable, and sufficient — until the moment a human is\n  doing the typing.\n</p>\n\n<div class=\"callout note\">\n  <span class=\"callout-label\">Core insight</span>\n  Exact match assumes the query is a precise, correctly-spelled substring of\n  the target. Human queries almost never are.\n</div>\n\n<h2>Three ways humans fail to type what they mean</h2>\n\n<h3>1. Typos and transpositions</h3>\n<p>\n  A developer types <code>comit</code> instead of <code>commit</code>.\n  They type <code>recieve</code> instead of <code>receive</code>.\n  Exact match returns nothing. But the intent is unambiguous.\n</p>\n\n<h3>2. Abbreviations</h3>\n<p>\n  A user types <code>usr/lcl</code> to find <code>/usr/local/bin</code>.\n  They type <code>MC</code> to find <code>MyComponent.tsx</code>.\n  No substring match exists, but the intent is clear: the query characters\n  all appear in the target, in order.\n</p>\n\n<h3>3. Fuzzy recall</h3>\n<p>\n  A user remembers a file is \"something about authentication, starts with\n  auth, has config in the name.\" They type <code>authcfg</code>.\n  The file is named <code>auth_config_loader.ts</code>.\n  Again: exact match fails, human intent succeeds.\n</p>\n\n<div class=\"demo\" id=\"demo-exact\">\n  <h3>Try it: Exact substring search</h3>\n  <p style=\"font-family:var(--sans);font-size:0.88rem;color:var(--muted);margin-bottom:0.75rem;\">\n    Type a query. See which candidates match using exact substring search.\n  </p>\n  <input type=\"text\" id=\"exact-input\" placeholder=\"try: comit, authcfg, usr/lcl\" autocomplete=\"off\" spellcheck=\"false\">\n  <div class=\"demo-results\" id=\"exact-results\"></div>\n</div>\n\n<h2>What the problem really is</h2>\n\n<p>\n  The challenge has two distinct parts, and most confusion about fuzzy search\n  comes from conflating them:\n</p>\n\n<ol>\n  <li>\n    <strong>Matching</strong> — deciding whether a candidate is \"close enough\"\n    to the query at all.\n  </li>\n  <li>\n    <strong>Ranking</strong> — when multiple candidates pass the threshold,\n    deciding which one the user probably meant.\n  </li>\n</ol>\n\n<p>\n  These require different machinery. A good fuzzy search algorithm solves\n  <em>both</em> — and the rest of this course shows how each design decision\n  targets one or the other.\n</p>\n\n<div class=\"callout insight\">\n  <span class=\"callout-label\">Preview of what's coming</span>\n  <strong>Edit distance</strong> solves matching: it gives us a number\n  representing how \"far apart\" two strings are. <strong>Scoring models</strong>\n  (gap penalties, consecutive bonuses, boundary bonuses) solve ranking:\n  they let us order results by likely intent.\n</div>\n\n<h2>What properties must a solution have?</h2>\n\n<p>Before building anything, it helps to list the requirements the solution\nmust satisfy. A fuzzy search algorithm should:</p>\n\n<ul>\n  <li><strong>Tolerate small typos</strong> — off-by-one character edits should not disqualify a match.</li>\n  <li><strong>Support abbreviations</strong> — all query characters appear in the target, in order (subsequence matching).</li>\n  <li><strong>Rank better matches first</strong> — <code>comit</code> → <code>commit</code> should rank above <code>comit</code> → <code>recommit_changes_v2.log</code>.</li>\n  <li><strong>Prefer compact matches</strong> — matching 4 consecutive characters should score higher than the same 4 characters scattered across a 200-character string.</li>\n  <li><strong>Prefer early and boundary matches</strong> — a match at the start of a word or path component feels more intentional than one buried in the middle.</li>\n</ul>\n\n<div class=\"callout warn\">\n  <span class=\"callout-label\">The tradeoff</span>\n  Every relaxation of exact match increases the number of results returned\n  (recall goes up). The scoring model is what keeps <em>precision</em> usable\n  by surfacing the right result first.\n</div>\n\n<h2>Quiz: Check your understanding</h2>\n\n<div class=\"quiz\" id=\"q1\">\n  <p class=\"quiz-question\">Q1. Exact substring search on the query <code>authcfg</code> against the target <code>auth_config_loader.ts</code> returns no match. What property does a fuzzy search algorithm need to find it?</p>\n  <ul class=\"quiz-options\">\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q1\" value=\"a\"> Tolerance for transpositions only</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q1\" value=\"b\"> Subsequence matching — all query chars appear in the target in order</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q1\" value=\"c\"> Phonetic similarity between query and target</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q1\" value=\"d\"> Case-insensitive exact matching</label></li>\n  </ul>\n  <button class=\"btn btn-primary\" onclick=\"checkQ('q1','b','Correct. The characters a-u-t-h-c-f-g all appear in auth_config_loader.ts in that order. This is subsequence matching — a core building block of fuzzy search.')\">Check</button>\n  <p class=\"quiz-feedback\" id=\"q1-feedback\"></p>\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"quiz\" id=\"q2\">\n  <p class=\"quiz-question\">Q2. Two candidates both pass the fuzzy match threshold for query <code>cfg</code>: <code>cfg.ts</code> and <code>app_configuration_manager.ts</code>. Which problem does the <em>scoring model</em> solve here?</p>\n  <ul class=\"quiz-options\">\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q2\" value=\"a\"> It decides whether either candidate matches at all</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q2\" value=\"b\"> It computes how many characters differ between query and candidate</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q2\" value=\"c\"> It ranks the candidates so the more likely intended result appears first</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q2\" value=\"d\"> It removes duplicate matches from the result set</label></li>\n  </ul>\n  <button class=\"btn btn-primary\" onclick=\"checkQ('q2','c','Correct. Matching decides yes/no. Scoring decides who comes first. cfg.ts should rank above app_configuration_manager.ts because the match is more compact and starts at a word boundary.')\">Check</button>\n  <p class=\"quiz-feedback\" id=\"q2-feedback\"></p>\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"quiz\" id=\"q3\">\n  <p class=\"quiz-question\">Q3. A fuzzy search that is very permissive (accepts almost anything) will have high recall but low precision. What is the role of the scoring model in addressing this tradeoff?</p>\n  <ul class=\"quiz-options\">\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q3\" value=\"a\"> It raises the match threshold to reduce the number of results</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q3\" value=\"b\"> It surfaces the most likely intended result first, making high recall usable</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q3\" value=\"c\"> It replaces the match threshold entirely</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q3\" value=\"d\"> It reduces the length of candidates to improve comparison speed</label></li>\n  </ul>\n  <button class=\"btn btn-primary\" onclick=\"checkQ('q3','b','Correct. You keep recall high so nothing is missed, but scoring ensures position #1 is almost always right. The user does not have to wade through noise.')\">Check</button>\n  <p class=\"quiz-feedback\" id=\"q3-feedback\"></p>\n</div>\n\n<hr>\n\n<h2>The curriculum ahead</h2>\n\n<p>Each of the next five lessons targets one specific design decision and the problem it solves:</p>\n\n<ul class=\"curriculum\">\n  <li><span class=\"num current\">01</span> <span class=\"current\">The Problem — why exact match fails (this lesson)</span></li>\n  <li><span class=\"num\">02</span> <a href=\"0002-edit-distance.html\">Edit Distance — formalising \"closeness\" with Levenshtein distance</a></li>\n  <li><span class=\"num locked\">03</span> <span class=\"locked\">Computing Edit Distance — the DP table and recurrence relation</span></li>\n  <li><span class=\"num locked\">04</span> <span class=\"locked\">From Distance to Search — threshold filtering and normalisation</span></li>\n  <li><span class=\"num locked\">05</span> <span class=\"locked\">The Scoring Model — gap penalties, consecutive and boundary bonuses (fzy)</span></li>\n  <li><span class=\"num locked\">06</span> <span class=\"locked\">The Bitap Algorithm — bit-parallel matching for performance</span></li>\n</ul>\n\n<div class=\"primary-source\">\n  <span class=\"label\">Primary source for this lesson</span>\n  <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Approximate_string_matching\" target=\"_blank\">\n    Approximate string matching — Wikipedia\n  </a>\n  — Read the intro and \"Problem formulation\" sections. It frames the\n  problem space precisely and introduces the vocabulary used in all subsequent\n  algorithm literature.\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"ask-teacher\">\n  💬 <strong>Have a question?</strong> Ask your teacher directly — type your question in the chat and I'll explain, rephrase, or go deeper on anything from this lesson.\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"lesson-nav\">\n  <span></span>\n  <span class=\"center\">Fuzzy Search · Lesson 1 of 6</span>\n  <a href=\"0002-edit-distance.html\">Next: Edit Distance →</a>\n</div>\n\n<script>\n  // ── Exact-search demo ──────────────────────────────────\n  const CANDIDATES = [\n    'commit',\n    'auth_config_loader.ts',\n    '/usr/local/bin',\n    'MyComponent.tsx',\n    'receive',\n    'recommit_changes_v2.log',\n    'cfg.ts',\n    'app_configuration_manager.ts',\n  ];\n\n  const exactInput   = document.getElementById('exact-input');\n  const exactResults = document.getElementById('exact-results');\n\n  function runExact(query) {\n    if (!query) { exactResults.innerHTML = ''; return; }\n    const q = query.toLowerCase();\n    const lines = CANDIDATES.map(c => {\n      const hit = c.toLowerCase().includes(q);\n      const escaped = c.replace(/</g, '&lt;');\n      if (hit) {\n        const highlighted = escaped.replace(\n          new RegExp(q.replace(/[.*+?^${}()|[\\]\\\\]/g, '\\\\$&'), 'gi'),\n          m => `<span class=\"hit\">${m}</span>`\n        );\n        return `<div class=\"match\">✓ ${highlighted}</div>`;\n      }\n      return `<div class=\"no-match\">✗ ${escaped}</div>`;\n    });\n    exactResults.innerHTML = lines.join('');\n  }\n\n  exactInput.addEventListener('input', e => runExact(e.target.value));\n\n  // ── Quiz logic ─────────────────────────────────────────\n  function checkQ(id, correct, explanation) {\n    const selected = document.querySelector(`input[name=\"${id}\"]:checked`);\n    const feedback = document.getElementById(`${id}-feedback`);\n    if (!selected) { feedback.textContent = 'Please select an answer.'; feedback.className = 'quiz-feedback'; return; }\n\n    document.querySelectorAll(`input[name=\"${id}\"]`).forEach(r => {\n      const lbl = r.closest('label');\n      if (r.value === correct) lbl.classList.add('correct');\n      else if (r.checked)      lbl.classList.add('wrong');\n      r.disabled = true;\n    });\n\n    if (selected.value === correct) {\n      feedback.textContent = '✓ ' + explanation;\n      feedback.className = 'quiz-feedback correct';\n    } else {\n      feedback.textContent = '✗ Not quite. ' + explanation;\n      feedback.className = 'quiz-feedback wrong';\n    }\n  }\n</script>\n\n</body>\n</html>\n"
}
```

Input: {
  "file_path": "/work/random/ai-teach-test/lessons/0002-edit-distance.html",
  "content": "<!DOCTYPE html>\n<html lang=\"en\">\n<head>\n  <meta charset=\"UTF-8\">\n  <meta name=\"viewport\" content=\"width=device-width, initial-scale=1.0\">\n  <title>Lesson 2 — Edit Distance: Formalising \"Closeness\"</title>\n  <link rel=\"stylesheet\" href=\"../assets/style.css\">\n</head>\n<body>\n\n<div class=\"lesson-header\">\n  <div class=\"lesson-meta\">Fuzzy Search · Lesson 2 of 6</div>\n  <h1>Edit Distance: Formalising \"Closeness\"</h1>\n  <p class=\"lesson-subtitle\">The design decision: define a precise metric for how far apart two strings are</p>\n</div>\n\n<h2>The problem this lesson solves</h2>\n\n<p>\n  In Lesson 1 we established that fuzzy search needs to decide whether a\n  candidate is \"close enough\" to the query. But <em>close enough</em> is not\n  an algorithm — it's a feeling. To build a machine that makes this decision\n  reliably, we need a <strong>number</strong>.\n</p>\n\n<div class=\"callout note\">\n  <span class=\"callout-label\">Design decision</span>\n  Define a distance metric between two strings: a single integer that\n  captures how many primitive editing steps are needed to transform one\n  string into the other. This metric is called <strong>edit distance</strong>\n  (or <strong>Levenshtein distance</strong>, after Vladimir Levenshtein, 1965).\n</div>\n\n<p>\n  Once we have this number, matching becomes a threshold check:\n  <em>\"accept the candidate if its edit distance from the query is ≤ k.\"</em>\n  The choice of k is still a policy decision, but the distance itself is\n  objective and computable.\n</p>\n\n<h2>The three primitive operations</h2>\n\n<p>\n  Edit distance counts the <strong>minimum number of single-character\n  edits</strong> needed to turn string A into string B. The allowed edits are:\n</p>\n\n<div style=\"overflow-x:auto;margin:1.5rem 0;\">\n<table class=\"dp-table\" style=\"width:100%;\">\n  <thead>\n    <tr><th>Operation</th><th>Example</th><th>Cost</th></tr>\n  </thead>\n  <tbody>\n    <tr><td><strong>Insert</strong> a character</td><td><code>cat</code> → <code>ca<em>r</em>t</code></td><td>1</td></tr>\n    <tr><td><strong>Delete</strong> a character</td><td><code>c<em>o</em>mit</code> → <code>cmit</code></td><td>1</td></tr>\n    <tr><td><strong>Substitute</strong> a character</td><td><code><em>k</em>itten</code> → <code><em>s</em>itten</code></td><td>1</td></tr>\n  </tbody>\n</table>\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"callout note\">\n  <span class=\"callout-label\">Why these three?</span>\n  These are the three irreducible ways a single character can be wrong.\n  Insert covers missing characters; delete covers extra characters;\n  substitute covers wrong characters. Together they span all\n  single-character divergences between two strings.\n</div>\n\n<h2>Calculating edit distance by hand</h2>\n\n<p>\n  Let's find the edit distance between <code>comit</code> (a typo) and\n  <code>commit</code> (the intended word).\n</p>\n\n<pre>comit   →   commit\n\nStep 1: comit → commit    (insert 'm' after the first 'm')\n                           cost: 1\n\nTotal edit distance: 1</pre>\n\n<p>Now a harder example: <code>kitten</code> → <code>sitting</code>.</p>\n\n<pre>kitten  →  sitting\n\nStep 1: kitten  → sitten   (substitute 'k' → 's')\nStep 2: sitten  → sittin   (substitute 'e' → 'i')\nStep 3: sittin  → sitting  (insert 'g' at the end)\n\nTotal edit distance: 3</pre>\n\n<div class=\"callout warn\">\n  <span class=\"callout-label\">The key word is \"minimum\"</span>\n  There are many sequences of edits that transform one string into another.\n  Edit distance is the <em>minimum</em> such count. Finding this minimum\n  naively (by trying all paths) is exponentially expensive — which is exactly\n  the problem Lesson 3 solves with dynamic programming.\n</div>\n\n<h2>Why the minimum matters for fuzzy search</h2>\n\n<p>\n  We want edit distance to represent the <em>most charitable interpretation</em>\n  of the user's intent. If a human could have produced the query in one\n  keystroke slip, it should cost 1 — not 5. Using the minimum edit count\n  correctly captures the \"how many mistakes did this take?\" intuition.\n</p>\n\n<h2>Try it: Edit distance explorer</h2>\n\n<div class=\"demo\" id=\"demo-edit\">\n  <h3>Edit distance between two strings</h3>\n  <p style=\"font-family:var(--sans);font-size:0.88rem;color:var(--muted);margin-bottom:0.9rem;\">\n    Type a source and target string. The edit distance is computed live using\n    the Wagner–Fischer algorithm (you'll understand this fully in Lesson 3).\n  </p>\n  <div style=\"display:flex;gap:0.75rem;flex-wrap:wrap;\">\n    <div style=\"flex:1;min-width:140px;\">\n      <label style=\"font-family:var(--sans);font-size:0.8rem;color:var(--muted);display:block;margin-bottom:0.3rem;\">Source</label>\n      <input type=\"text\" id=\"src-input\" value=\"comit\" autocomplete=\"off\" spellcheck=\"false\">\n    </div>\n    <div style=\"flex:1;min-width:140px;\">\n      <label style=\"font-family:var(--sans);font-size:0.8rem;color:var(--muted);display:block;margin-bottom:0.3rem;\">Target</label>\n      <input type=\"text\" id=\"tgt-input\" value=\"commit\" autocomplete=\"off\" spellcheck=\"false\">\n    </div>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"demo-results\" id=\"edit-result\" style=\"margin-top:1rem;font-family:var(--sans);font-size:1rem;\"></div>\n</div>\n\n<h2>A note on variants</h2>\n\n<p>\n  Levenshtein distance (insert, delete, substitute) is the most common\n  formulation, but two variants are worth knowing:\n</p>\n\n<ul>\n  <li>\n    <strong>Damerau–Levenshtein</strong> — adds a fourth operation:\n    <em>transposition</em> (swapping two adjacent characters, e.g. <code>teh</code> → <code>the</code>).\n    This catches a very common class of typo. Many real fuzzy search\n    implementations use this variant.\n  </li>\n  <li>\n    <strong>Hamming distance</strong> — only counts substitutions, and\n    requires strings of equal length. Simpler, but too rigid for search.\n  </li>\n</ul>\n\n<div class=\"callout insight\">\n  <span class=\"callout-label\">Design insight</span>\n  The choice of which operations to allow is itself a design decision.\n  Levenshtein without transposition will give <code>teh</code> a distance of 2\n  from <code>the</code> (delete + insert). With Damerau's extension, it's 1.\n  The right choice depends on the error model for your users.\n</div>\n\n<h2>Quiz: Check your understanding</h2>\n\n<div class=\"quiz\" id=\"q1\">\n  <p class=\"quiz-question\">Q1. What is the Levenshtein distance between <code>recieve</code> and <code>receive</code>?</p>\n  <ul class=\"quiz-options\">\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q1\" value=\"a\"> 0 — they are the same string</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q1\" value=\"b\"> 2 — delete 'i', insert 'i' in the right place</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q1\" value=\"c\"> 1 — one transposition swaps adjacent 'i' and 'e'</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q1\" value=\"d\"> 3 — three substitutions are required</label></li>\n  </ul>\n  <button class=\"btn btn-primary\" onclick=\"checkQ('q1','c','Correct. recieve → receive is a single transposition of adjacent characters e↔i. Under Levenshtein (no transpositions) it costs 2; under Damerau-Levenshtein it costs 1. This distinction matters in practice.')\">Check</button>\n  <p class=\"quiz-feedback\" id=\"q1-feedback\"></p>\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"quiz\" id=\"q2\">\n  <p class=\"quiz-question\">Q2. Why does edit distance use the <em>minimum</em> number of operations, rather than any valid sequence?</p>\n  <ul class=\"quiz-options\">\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q2\" value=\"a\"> To make computation faster by stopping at the first valid path</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q2\" value=\"b\"> To represent the most charitable interpretation of the user's intent</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q2\" value=\"c\"> Because longer edit sequences are always invalid</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q2\" value=\"d\"> To ensure the distance is always a positive integer</label></li>\n  </ul>\n  <button class=\"btn btn-primary\" onclick=\"checkQ('q2','b','Correct. A user who typed \"comit\" made one slip. Counting the minimum operations reflects the fewest errors needed, which best models actual human intent.')\">Check</button>\n  <p class=\"quiz-feedback\" id=\"q2-feedback\"></p>\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"quiz\" id=\"q3\">\n  <p class=\"quiz-question\">Q3. Which edit operation is NOT part of standard Levenshtein distance?</p>\n  <ul class=\"quiz-options\">\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q3\" value=\"a\"> Insert a character into the string</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q3\" value=\"b\"> Delete a character from the string</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q3\" value=\"c\"> Transpose two adjacent characters</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q3\" value=\"d\"> Substitute one character for another</label></li>\n  </ul>\n  <button class=\"btn btn-primary\" onclick=\"checkQ('q3','c','Correct. Transposition is the Damerau extension. Standard Levenshtein only has insert, delete, and substitute.')\">Check</button>\n  <p class=\"quiz-feedback\" id=\"q3-feedback\"></p>\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"quiz\" id=\"q4\">\n  <p class=\"quiz-question\">Q4. Edit distance between <code>abc</code> and <code>xyz</code> is:</p>\n  <ul class=\"quiz-options\">\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q4\" value=\"a\"> 6 — delete all of abc, insert all of xyz</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q4\" value=\"b\"> 3 — substitute each character once</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q4\" value=\"c\"> 0 — both strings have the same length</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q4\" value=\"d\"> 2 — insert x at start, delete c at end</label></li>\n  </ul>\n  <button class=\"btn btn-primary\" onclick=\"checkQ('q4','b','Correct. abc → xbc (sub a→x), xbc → xyc (sub b→y), xyc → xyz (sub c→z). Three substitutions. Minimum is 3.')\">Check</button>\n  <p class=\"quiz-feedback\" id=\"q4-feedback\"></p>\n</div>\n\n<hr>\n\n<div class=\"primary-source\">\n  <span class=\"label\">Primary source for this lesson</span>\n  <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Levenshtein_distance\" target=\"_blank\">\n    Levenshtein distance — Wikipedia\n  </a>\n  — Read the \"Definition\" and \"Example\" sections. The recurrence relation\n  defined there is exactly what Lesson 3 will implement as a DP table.\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"ask-teacher\">\n  💬 <strong>Have a question?</strong> Ask your teacher — type in the chat and I'll explain, rephrase, or go deeper on anything from this lesson.\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"lesson-nav\">\n  <a href=\"0001-the-problem.html\">← Lesson 1: The Problem</a>\n  <span class=\"center\">Fuzzy Search · Lesson 2 of 6</span>\n  <a href=\"0003-dp-table.html\">Next: Computing Edit Distance →</a>\n</div>\n\n<script>\n  // ── Edit distance (Wagner-Fischer) ─────────────────────\n  function editDistance(a, b) {\n    const m = a.length, n = b.length;\n    const dp = Array.from({length: m + 1}, (_, i) =>\n      Array.from({length: n + 1}, (_, j) => i === 0 ? j : j === 0 ? i : 0)\n    );\n    for (let i = 1; i <= m; i++) {\n      for (let j = 1; j <= n; j++) {\n        if (a[i-1] === b[j-1]) {\n          dp[i][j] = dp[i-1][j-1];\n        } else {\n          dp[i][j] = 1 + Math.min(\n            dp[i-1][j],   // delete\n            dp[i][j-1],   // insert\n            dp[i-1][j-1]  // substitute\n          );\n        }\n      }\n    }\n    return dp[m][n];\n  }\n\n  function updateDemo() {\n    const src = document.getElementById('src-input').value;\n    const tgt = document.getElementById('tgt-input').value;\n    const result = document.getElementById('edit-result');\n    if (!src || !tgt) { result.textContent = ''; return; }\n    const d = editDistance(src, tgt);\n    const colour = d === 0 ? 'var(--success)' : d <= 2 ? 'var(--warn)' : 'var(--danger)';\n    result.innerHTML = `Edit distance: <strong style=\"color:${colour};font-size:1.4rem;\">${d}</strong>\n      <span style=\"color:var(--muted);font-size:0.85rem;margin-left:0.75rem;\">(${d} operation${d===1?'':'s'} needed)</span>`;\n  }\n\n  document.getElementById('src-input').addEventListener('input', updateDemo);\n  document.getElementById('tgt-input').addEventListener('input', updateDemo);\n  updateDemo();\n\n  // ── Quiz logic ─────────────────────────────────────────\n  function checkQ(id, correct, explanation) {\n    const selected = document.querySelector(`input[name=\"${id}\"]:checked`);\n    const feedback = document.getElementById(`${id}-feedback`);\n    if (!selected) { feedback.textContent = 'Please select an answer.'; feedback.className = 'quiz-feedback'; return; }\n\n    document.querySelectorAll(`input[name=\"${id}\"]`).forEach(r => {\n      const lbl = r.closest('label');\n      if (r.value === correct) lbl.classList.add('correct');\n      else if (r.checked)      lbl.classList.add('wrong');\n      r.disabled = true;\n    });\n\n    if (selected.value === correct) {\n      feedback.textContent = '✓ ' + explanation;\n      feedback.className = 'quiz-feedback correct';\n    } else {\n      feedback.textContent = '✗ Not quite. ' + explanation;\n      feedback.className = 'quiz-feedback wrong';\n    }\n  }\n</script>\n\n</body>\n</html>\n"
}

Opening in existing browser session.

Lesson 2 is open. Here's what it covers:

**The design decision this lesson justifies**: we need a *number* to represent closeness, not a gut feeling. That number is edit distance — the minimum single-character edits (insert, delete, substitute) to transform one string into another.

Key things you'll encounter:
- **The three operations** and why exactly those three (they span all single-character divergences)
- **Why minimum** — it's the most charitable interpretation of the user's intent
- **The live demo** — type any two strings and see the distance update in real time
- **The Damerau extension** — transposition as a fourth operation, and why it matters for real typos like `teh` → `the`
- **Four quiz questions** to lock in the concepts

Once you finish the quizzes, come back and I'll open Lesson 3: why computing edit distance naively is exponentially slow, and how the DP table solves it.

But <em>close enough</em> is not\n  an algorithm — it's a feeling. To build a machine that makes this decision\n  reliably, we need a <strong>number</strong>.\n</p>\n\n<div class=\"callout note\">\n  <span class=\"callout-label\">Design decision</span>\n  Define a distance metric between two strings: a single integer that\n  captures how many primitive editing steps are needed to transform one\n  string into the other. This metric is called <strong>edit distance</strong>\n  (or <strong>Levenshtein distance</strong>, after Vladimir Levenshtein, 1965).\n</div>\n\n<p>\n  Once we have this number, matching becomes a threshold check:\n  <em>\"accept the candidate if its edit distance from the query is ≤ k.\"</em>\n  The choice of k is still a policy decision, but the distance itself is\n  objective and computable.\n</p>\n\n<h2>The three primitive operations</h2>\n\n<p>\n  Edit distance counts the <strong>minimum number of single-character\n  edits</strong> needed to turn string A into string B. The allowed edits are:\n</p>\n\n<div style=\"overflow-x:auto;margin:1.5rem 0;\">\n<table class=\"dp-table\" style=\"width:100%;\">\n  <thead>\n    <tr><th>Operation</th><th>Example</th><th>Cost</th></tr>\n  </thead>\n  <tbody>\n    <tr><td><strong>Insert</strong> a character</td><td><code>cat</code> → <code>ca<em>r</em>t</code></td><td>1</td></tr>\n    <tr><td><strong>Delete</strong> a character</td><td><code>c<em>o</em>mit</code> → <code>cmit</code></td><td>1</td></tr>\n    <tr><td><strong>Substitute</strong> a character</td><td><code><em>k</em>itten</code> → <code><em>s</em>itten</code></td><td>1</td></tr>\n  </tbody>\n</table>\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"callout note\">\n  <span class=\"callout-label\">Why these three?</span>\n  These are the three irreducible ways a single character can be wrong.\n  Insert covers missing characters; delete covers extra characters;\n  substitute covers wrong characters. Together they span all\n  single-character divergences between two strings.\n</div>\n\n<h2>Calculating edit distance by hand</h2>\n\n<p>\n  Let's find the edit distance between <code>comit</code> (a typo) and\n  <code>commit</code> (the intended word).\n</p>\n\n<pre>comit   →   commit\n\nStep 1: comit → commit    (insert 'm' after the first 'm')\n                           cost: 1\n\nTotal edit distance: 1</pre>\n\n<p>Now a harder example: <code>kitten</code> → <code>sitting</code>.</p>\n\n<pre>kitten  →  sitting\n\nStep 1: kitten  → sitten   (substitute 'k' → 's')\nStep 2: sitten  → sittin   (substitute 'e' → 'i')\nStep 3: sittin  → sitting  (insert 'g' at the end)\n\nTotal edit distance: 3</pre>\n\n<div class=\"callout warn\">\n  <span class=\"callout-label\">The key word is \"minimum\"</span>\n  There are many sequences of edits that transform one string into another.\n  Edit distance is the <em>minimum</em> such count. Finding this minimum\n  naively (by trying all paths) is exponentially expensive — which is exactly\n  the problem Lesson 3 solves with dynamic programming.\n</div>\n\n<h2>Why the minimum matters for fuzzy search</h2>\n\n<p>\n  We want edit distance to represent the <em>most charitable interpretation</em>\n  of the user's intent. If a human could have produced the query in one\n  keystroke slip, it should cost 1 — not 5. Using the minimum edit count\n  correctly captures the \"how many mistakes did this take?\" intuition.\n</p>\n\n<h2>Try it: Edit distance explorer</h2>\n\n<div class=\"demo\" id=\"demo-edit\">\n  <h3>Edit distance between two strings</h3>\n  <p style=\"font-family:var(--sans);font-size:0.88rem;color:var(--muted);margin-bottom:0.9rem;\">\n    Type a source and target string. The edit distance is computed live using\n    the Wagner–Fischer algorithm (you'll understand this fully in Lesson 3).\n  </p>\n  <div style=\"display:flex;gap:0.75rem;flex-wrap:wrap;\">\n    <div style=\"flex:1;min-width:140px;\">\n      <label style=\"font-family:var(--sans);font-size:0.8rem;color:var(--muted);display:block;margin-bottom:0.3rem;\">Source</label>\n      <input type=\"text\" id=\"src-input\" value=\"comit\" autocomplete=\"off\" spellcheck=\"false\">\n    </div>\n    <div style=\"flex:1;min-width:140px;\">\n      <label style=\"font-family:var(--sans);font-size:0.8rem;color:var(--muted);display:block;margin-bottom:0.3rem;\">Target</label>\n      <input type=\"text\" id=\"tgt-input\" value=\"commit\" autocomplete=\"off\" spellcheck=\"false\">\n    </div>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"demo-results\" id=\"edit-result\" style=\"margin-top:1rem;font-family:var(--sans);font-size:1rem;\"></div>\n</div>\n\n<h2>A note on variants</h2>\n\n<p>\n  Levenshtein distance (insert, delete, substitute) is the most common\n  formulation, but two variants are worth knowing:\n</p>\n\n<ul>\n  <li>\n    <strong>Damerau–Levenshtein</strong> — adds a fourth operation:\n    <em>transposition</em> (swapping two adjacent characters, e.g. <code>teh</code> → <code>the</code>).\n    This catches a very common class of typo. Many real fuzzy search\n    implementations use this variant.\n  </li>\n  <li>\n    <strong>Hamming distance</strong> — only counts substitutions, and\n    requires strings of equal length. Simpler, but too rigid for search.\n  </li>\n</ul>\n\n<div class=\"callout insight\">\n  <span class=\"callout-label\">Design insight</span>\n  The choice of which operations to allow is itself a design decision.\n  Levenshtein without transposition will give <code>teh</code> a distance of 2\n  from <code>the</code> (delete + insert). With Damerau's extension, it's 1.\n  The right choice depends on the error model for your users.\n</div>\n\n<h2>Quiz: Check your understanding</h2>\n\n<div class=\"quiz\" id=\"q1\">\n  <p class=\"quiz-question\">Q1. What is the Levenshtein distance between <code>recieve</code> and <code>receive</code>?</p>\n  <ul class=\"quiz-options\">\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q1\" value=\"a\"> 0 — they are the same string</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q1\" value=\"b\"> 2 — delete 'i', insert 'i' in the right place</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q1\" value=\"c\"> 1 — one transposition swaps adjacent 'i' and 'e'</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q1\" value=\"d\"> 3 — three substitutions are required</label></li>\n  </ul>\n  <button class=\"btn btn-primary\" onclick=\"checkQ('q1','c','Correct. recieve → receive is a single transposition of adjacent characters e↔i. Under Levenshtein (no transpositions) it costs 2; under Damerau-Levenshtein it costs 1. This distinction matters in practice.')\">Check</button>\n  <p class=\"quiz-feedback\" id=\"q1-feedback\"></p>\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"quiz\" id=\"q2\">\n  <p class=\"quiz-question\">Q2. Why does edit distance use the <em>minimum</em> number of operations, rather than any valid sequence?</p>\n  <ul class=\"quiz-options\">\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q2\" value=\"a\"> To make computation faster by stopping at the first valid path</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q2\" value=\"b\"> To represent the most charitable interpretation of the user's intent</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q2\" value=\"c\"> Because longer edit sequences are always invalid</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q2\" value=\"d\"> To ensure the distance is always a positive integer</label></li>\n  </ul>\n  <button class=\"btn btn-primary\" onclick=\"checkQ('q2','b','Correct. A user who typed \"comit\" made one slip. Counting the minimum operations reflects the fewest errors needed, which best models actual human intent.')\">Check</button>\n  <p class=\"quiz-feedback\" id=\"q2-feedback\"></p>\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"quiz\" id=\"q3\">\n  <p class=\"quiz-question\">Q3. Which edit operation is NOT part of standard Levenshtein distance?</p>\n  <ul class=\"quiz-options\">\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q3\" value=\"a\"> Insert a character into the string</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q3\" value=\"b\"> Delete a character from the string</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q3\" value=\"c\"> Transpose two adjacent characters</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q3\" value=\"d\"> Substitute one character for another</label></li>\n  </ul>\n  <button class=\"btn btn-primary\" onclick=\"checkQ('q3','c','Correct. Transposition is the Damerau extension. Standard Levenshtein only has insert, delete, and substitute.')\">Check</button>\n  <p class=\"quiz-feedback\" id=\"q3-feedback\"></p>\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"quiz\" id=\"q4\">\n  <p class=\"quiz-question\">Q4. Edit distance between <code>abc</code> and <code>xyz</code> is:</p>\n  <ul class=\"quiz-options\">\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q4\" value=\"a\"> 6 — delete all of abc, insert all of xyz</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q4\" value=\"b\"> 3 — substitute each character once</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q4\" value=\"c\"> 0 — both strings have the same length</label></li>\n    <li><label><input type=\"radio\" name=\"q4\" value=\"d\"> 2 — insert x at start, delete c at end</label></li>\n  </ul>\n  <button class=\"btn btn-primary\" onclick=\"checkQ('q4','b','Correct. abc → xbc (sub a→x), xbc → xyc (sub b→y), xyc → xyz (sub c→z). Three substitutions. Minimum is 3.')\">Check</button>\n  <p class=\"quiz-feedback\" id=\"q4-feedback\"></p>\n</div>\n\n<hr>\n\n<div class=\"primary-source\">\n  <span class=\"label\">Primary source for this lesson</span>\n  <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Levenshtein_distance\" target=\"_blank\">\n    Levenshtein distance — Wikipedia\n  </a>\n  — Read the \"Definition\" and \"Example\" sections. The recurrence relation\n  defined there is exactly what Lesson 3 will implement as a DP table.\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"ask-teacher\">\n  💬 <strong>Have a question?</strong> Ask your teacher — type in the chat and I'll explain, rephrase, or go deeper on anything from this lesson.\n</div>\n\n<div class=\"lesson-nav\">\n  <a href=\"0001-the-problem.html\">← Lesson 1: The Problem</a>\n  <span class=\"center\">Fuzzy Search · Lesson 2 of 6</span>\n  <a href=\"0003-dp-table.html\">Next: Computing Edit Distance →</a>\n</div>\n\n<script>\n  // ── Edit distance (Wagner-Fischer) ─────────────────────\n  function editDistance(a, b) {\n    const m = a.length, n = b.length;\n    const dp = Array.from({length: m + 1}, (_, i) =>\n      Array.from({length: n + 1}, (_, j) => i === 0 ? j : j === 0 ? i : 0)\n    );\n    for (let i = 1; i <= m; i++) {\n      for (let j = 1; j <= n; j++) {\n        if (a[i-1] === b[j-1]) {\n          dp[i][j] = dp[i-1][j-1];\n        } else {\n          dp[i][j] = 1 + Math.min(\n            dp[i-1][j],   // delete\n            dp[i][j-1],   // insert\n            dp[i-1][j-1]  // substitute\n          );\n        }\n      }\n    }\n    return dp[m][n];\n  }\n\n  function updateDemo() {\n    const src = document.getElementById('src-input').value;\n    const tgt = document.getElementById('tgt-input').value;\n    const result = document.getElementById('edit-result');\n    if (!src || !tgt) { result.textContent = ''; return; }\n    const d = editDistance(src, tgt);\n    const colour = d === 0 ? 'var(--success)' : d <= 2 ? 'var(--warn)' : 'var(--danger)';\n    result.innerHTML = `Edit distance: <strong style=\"color:${colour};font-size:1.4rem;\">${d}</strong>\n      <span style=\"color:var(--muted);font-size:0.85rem;margin-left:0.75rem;\">(${d} operation${d===1?'':'s'} needed)</span>`;\n  }\n\n  document.getElementById('src-input').addEventListener('input', updateDemo);\n  document.getElementById('tgt-input').addEventListener('input', updateDemo);\n  updateDemo();\n\n  // ── Quiz logic ─────────────────────────────────────────\n  function checkQ(id, correct, explanation) {\n    const selected = document.querySelector(`input[name=\"${id}\"]:checked`);\n    const feedback = document.getElementById(`${id}-feedback`);\n    if (!selected) { feedback.textContent = 'Please select an answer.'; feedback.className = 'quiz-feedback'; return; }\n\n    document.querySelectorAll(`input[name=\"${id}\"]`).forEach(r => {\n      const lbl = r.closest('label');\n      if (r.value === correct) lbl.classList.add('correct');\n      else if (r.checked)      lbl.classList.add('wrong');\n      r.disabled = true;\n    });\n\n    if (selected.value === correct) {\n      feedback.textContent = '✓ ' + explanation;\n      feedback.className = 'quiz-feedback correct';\n    } else {\n      feedback.textContent = '✗ Not quite. ' + explanation;\n      feedback.className = 'quiz-feedback wrong';\n    }\n  }\n</script>\n\n</body>\n</html>\n"
}
```
